# Story 0000056 – Users can use Copilot as a first-class chat provider with shared Agent Flags and defaults

## Implementation Plan

This is a list of steps that must be followed whilst working on a story. The first step of any plan is to copy this file to a new markdown document that starts with an index padded with zeroes starting at 1 and the title of the story. eg: ./planning/0000001-initial-skeleton-setup.md
Create (or reuse if it already exists) the feature branch for this phase using the established naming convention (for example `feature/<number>-<Title>`).
The Description, Acceptance Criteria & Out Of Scope sections must be fully documented to the point where a very junior, inexperienced developer who has never seen the product before would be able to read and understand what we want to do and why.
The Questions sections should be populated by an AI at the start of the planning phase, as soon as the initial description is provided. As these questions get answered, the questions should be removed and relevent information should be added to the other sections. The Questions section must be empty before creating tasks.

### Description

The product already has three chat providers in some form, but they do not feel equally supported from a user perspective. Codex currently has the strongest integration because it has repo-owned chat defaults, Codex-specific chat controls in the UI, and better alignment between the normal chat page and the MCP chat interface. GitHub Copilot support exists, but it is still a thinner integration. The page can select Copilot as a provider, yet Copilot does not currently receive the same tool wiring, the same default-config story, or the same provider-specific UI treatment that Codex receives.

This story closes that gap by making Copilot a first-class chat provider in the same product surfaces where Codex already feels strong, while also cleaning up the chat-provider architecture so it is no longer overly Codex-shaped. The user should be able to choose Copilot as the default chat provider, have the default chat model come from a provider-specific chat config file seeded by the app into the repo-local provider folder, use tools through the chat page and the chat MCP interface, and see a provider-neutral `Agent Flags` panel that shows only the options the selected provider actually supports.

The key design decision for this story is that the default-provider selector remains a single environment variable, but the default model no longer comes from a second shared environment variable. The environment variable `CODEINFO_CHAT_DEFAULT_PROVIDER` should remain the single top-level default selector. Once that provider is chosen, the server should load the selected provider's repo-owned chat config file and read the default model from there. This removes the need for `CODEINFO_CHAT_DEFAULT_MODEL` and avoids a confusing split where the provider comes from one place and the actual model comes from somewhere else.

The chosen config layout is intentionally symmetrical at the application layer:

- `codex/chat/config.toml` remains the repo-owned Codex chat-defaults file;
- `copilot/chat/config.toml` is added as a repo-owned Copilot chat-defaults file;
- `lmstudio/chat/config.toml` is added as a repo-owned LM Studio chat-defaults file.

These files are product-owned chat-default contracts seeded by the application into repo-local provider folders. They are not claims that all three providers natively read the same file shape themselves. Codex already has its own native runtime config under `codex/config.toml`. Copilot keeps its own auth and runtime home under `copilot/`. LM Studio keeps its own provider-specific runtime behavior. This story is about one consistent product-level defaults layer that the server reads and translates into each provider runtime, not about forcing every provider to adopt Codex's native on-disk contract.

The user has also now chosen that this product-owned defaults layer should stay normalized across providers instead of drifting into three unrelated local file shapes. In practice, that means `codex/chat/config.toml`, `copilot/chat/config.toml`, and `lmstudio/chat/config.toml` should all use one shared product-owned TOML format where provider-specific fields are added only when they are genuinely needed. Keeping the files in TOML preserves the current repo convention while still letting the server own one consistent parser, validator, and seeding path for all three providers.

That distinction matters most for Copilot. The user has chosen that Copilot auth and runtime state must stay under the existing `copilot/` home contract rather than moving into `copilot/chat/`. The new `copilot/chat/config.toml` file should therefore be treated as a repo-owned chat-defaults file only. It must not become Copilot's real runtime `configDir`, because doing that would risk separating chat defaults from the auth and state that the SDK and CLI already expect to live in the Copilot home.

The Copilot auth-home contract also now needs one explicit fragility repair. Live runtime evidence has shown that Copilot-managed files under that home can be rewritten in a JSON-with-comments style and that the current auth bootstrap path is too brittle because it still assumes one strict JSON artifact and one stale settings location. This story therefore now includes the in-scope work to make Copilot authentication persistence resilient to Copilot-managed JSONC artifacts, move the plaintext-persistence source of truth onto the live settings artifact the runtime actually uses, and stop blocking device auth just because a Copilot-managed compatibility file is commented or tool-owned.

The user has also now chosen that these provider folders should be seeded automatically rather than depending on a manually checked-in file for each provider. That means the local bootstrap path that already creates required repo-local folders for Codex and Copilot should be extended to create the LM Studio chat-defaults folder as well. The repo's gitignore and Docker build-context ignore rules should also keep the repo-local provider folders out of Git and out of image build inputs, including the new `lmstudio/` folder, so runtime defaults and local provider state do not create accidental source-control noise or get copied into Docker builds.

The remaining LM Studio folder decision is also now fixed. In this story, the new `lmstudio/` folder is only an app-managed seeded chat-defaults folder. It is not a new LM Studio runtime-state home. That means this story can seed `lmstudio/chat/config.toml` and keep it out of Git without also taking on a larger contract around LM Studio-native state, LM Studio-native MCP files, or a new Docker-mounted provider home for LM Studio itself. If the product later wants LM Studio-native runtime files in a repo-local home, that should be treated as a follow-up story rather than being silently folded into this one.

The same principle applies to tool support. Research and repo evidence show that Copilot can support tools through the SDK session layer, but the current repository does not actually wire that path up in normal chat execution. This story should therefore make app-managed tool parity the primary mechanism. In practice that means the server should construct Copilot tools from the repository's own trusted tool layer and pass them into Copilot session creation, rather than depending on provider-native MCP file discovery as the main product contract. Native provider-specific MCP config may still exist, but it is not the primary parity mechanism for this story.

The chat-page user experience also needs to become provider-neutral. Today the page has a `Codex flags` panel, uses Codex-specific defaults, and sends a `codexFlags` payload only for Codex. That architecture is too narrow now that the product supports Codex, Copilot, and LM Studio. This story should replace the Codex-only control path with a provider-neutral `Agent Flags` contract. The selected provider should advertise which flags or provider-specific options are supported, what their defaults are, and whether they are editable. The client should then render only those controls. This means Codex may still show the richest set of editable controls, while Copilot or LM Studio may show fewer. That is acceptable. The important rule is that the UI must not invent fake controls for providers that do not truly support them.

This story also now includes a dependency-refresh pass for the chat providers that most affect that contract. The current repository is behind on the published Codex package versions, so implementation should update `@openai/codex` and `@openai/codex-sdk` to the latest published versions verified at implementation time. The GitHub Copilot SDK version should also be rechecked during implementation so the story can move to the latest published Copilot API if a newer package is available by then. The LM Studio SDK version should likewise be rechecked during implementation, but because it was already current when this requirement was added, the story only needs to bump `@lmstudio/sdk` if a newer release exists at implementation time rather than forcing churn for its own sake. The reason that dependency refresh belongs in this story is that newer Codex releases expose a broader config surface than the app currently models, and the product also wants the Copilot integration reviewed against the latest published SDK contract rather than against a stale assumption.

The user has also chosen the preferred shape of that provider-neutral `Agent Flags` contract. Each provider implementation should expose a common option-descriptor method that returns a JSON array of the provider's exposed options. Each array item should represent one exposed option and include at least: a stable option key, a human-facing label, the UI control type the client should render, the supported values or value bounds, a hardcoded seed default used when the provider chat config is first created, and the resolved default currently loaded from that provider's `chat/config.toml`. The contract should preserve both the original seed default and the currently resolved config-backed default instead of collapsing them into one field, so bootstrap behavior and later operator edits stay visible and testable.

That option-descriptor contract should also be model-aware rather than provider-only. Some options, such as Codex and Copilot reasoning effort, can depend on the selected model instead of only the provider. Because of that, the shared provider option method should accept the selected model when resolving the available options and their effective defaults. The user has now chosen that the product should not fetch those flags through a second independent API just for flags. Instead, the server should return one combined models-and-flags payload where common provider flags are returned once and model-specific differences are returned as per-model overrides or capability narrowing inside the same response. In practice, the client should refresh that combined payload when the user changes provider, and use the selected model's override data when the user changes model, so the UI always reflects the actual provider-plus-model contract without splitting models and flags across separate fetches.

The client should use that server-provided option-descriptor JSON as the source of truth for building the `Agent Flags` controls rather than keeping provider-specific UI branching in the page. The initial values shown to the user should come from the resolved defaults loaded from the provider's current `chat/config.toml`, not directly from the hardcoded seed defaults, because the local config may already have been edited. Once the user changes those options in the UI, the selected values should be sent with each normal chat request so the request contract remains explicit and stateless at send time even though the defaults were seeded from provider-local config. The MCP chat interface remains out of scope for this flags payload in this story and should continue to rely on provider and model defaults only.

The user has also now chosen the first supported editable option sets for the new providers. Copilot should not pretend to have the exact Codex flag set. Instead, its first supported editable options should be the real Copilot-aligned options the repository can honestly support now: reasoning effort and one tool-access setting. That tool-access setting should be a product-level abstraction that controls the built-in Copilot tool and MCP surface as a whole, even though the SDK maps it through allowlist or denylist fields rather than through one native enum. The first visible Copilot `Agent Flags` should therefore stay intentionally smaller than the raw SDK surface. `workingDirectory` should stay out of scope for this story because the SDK already falls back to the current process working directory when no override is supplied, and the product does not currently need a second UI control for that behavior. Copilot permission behavior should also stay out of the visible `Agent Flags` surface for this story. The product wants Copilot to run without interruption in the containerized environment, so the first implementation should treat permissive non-interrupting approval as an implementation/defaults decision by using the SDK's `approveAll`-style permission path or an equivalent always-approve handler. Fine-grained Copilot approval controls and per-server MCP toggles can be follow-up stories if the product later wants them.

The user has also now chosen the exact first-pass shape of that single Copilot tool-access control. It should start with just `On` and `Off`, not a middle mode. `On` should map to the normal allowed Copilot tool surface for this product, and `Off` should map to no tool access. That keeps the first Copilot UI very easy to understand while still leaving room for a future story to add a safer middle mode if the product later wants one.

LM Studio should also get real editable options in this story, but they should be LM Studio-native generation or tool options rather than copied Codex labels. The initial LM Studio set should therefore be built around native generation or tool controls such as temperature, max tokens, context overflow handling, and tool access. This story should not add fake LM Studio controls that merely look like Codex settings without matching LM Studio behavior underneath.

The Codex side of that option contract is also now more precise than the current UI. The first provider-neutral Codex option set should include sandbox mode, approval policy, reasoning effort, reasoning summary, verbosity, network access, and web search mode. `modelReasoningSummary` and `modelVerbosity` are newly in scope because current Codex releases expose them as simple, user-understandable dropdowns, and they fit naturally into the new option-descriptor contract. The old boolean `webSearchEnabled` should be replaced by a real `webSearchMode` option so the product can express `disabled`, `cached`, and `live` instead of collapsing everything into on or off. The seeded Codex chat config should use the most permissive supported default for that field, which means `live`.

Approval behavior also needs an explicit simplification boundary. The old flat `on-failure` choice should be removed from the supported editable Codex UI for this story, and the first supported Codex approval choices should be limited to the remaining simple contract the user wants to keep. Newer Codex releases support a richer granular approval structure, but that is intentionally out of scope here because it would turn one dropdown into a larger multi-field permissions design. The story should therefore refresh Codex to the newer library versions without taking on the full granular-approval UI at the same time.

The same “simple where honest” rule applies to Codex network access. Newer Codex config expresses this as `sandbox_workspace_write.network_access`, which is still just a boolean. That means the product can keep the simple `networkAccessEnabled` flag in this story as long as it is documented and implemented as the workspace-write network-access toggle rather than as a provider-wide permission master switch. The seeded Codex default for that flag should be permissive and default to `true`.

This newer Codex contract also has a migration implication for existing local repos. Older local `codex/chat/config.toml` files may still contain removed values such as the old `on-failure` approval mode or the old boolean-style web-search setting. The user has chosen that this story should handle those older local configs by normalizing them forward and warning clearly, rather than by failing chat until the operator edits the local file by hand.

Changing Agent Flags should keep the user in the same conversation rather than automatically starting a fresh conversation. This matches the current Codex behavior in the chat page, where provider and model changes create a new conversation for the next send but flag changes do not. For this story, provider-neutral Agent Flags should preserve that existing interaction model: a flag change affects later sends in the current conversation instead of resetting the visible transcript.

Provider and model changes need equally explicit state rules. In this story they remain next-send mode switches rather than same-conversation mutations: when the user changes provider or model, the active Agent Flags draft should be rebuilt from the newly selected provider/model contract, any now-incompatible hidden flags should be cleared from the draft immediately, and the next send must not reuse the prior conversation's provider-specific persisted state such as Codex `threadId`. If the user selects an existing conversation from history after staging a provider/model change, the restored conversation state wins and the unsent next-send draft must not bleed into that restored conversation.

This story also extends the same parity principle to the MCP chat interface. The current `codebase_question` MCP tool still hardcodes provider support to Codex and LM Studio. Users want Copilot to feel equally usable when chat is driven through the MCP surface, not only when it is driven through the browser page. This story therefore includes provider-contract work so Copilot can be selected through the chat MCP interface anywhere that the current chat provider contract is meant to be shared.

Another important contract decision is that fallback behavior remains provider-first. If `CODEINFO_CHAT_DEFAULT_PROVIDER` points to a provider that is unavailable or misconfigured, the server should fall back to the next available provider using that fallback provider's own `chat/config.toml` default model. This story should not preserve a hidden hardcoded or env-driven default-model back door once provider-local config files become the source of truth. If a provider-specific chat config file is missing, unreadable, or invalid, that should be treated as a provider misconfiguration that can trigger warning logs and provider fallback rather than silently selecting a surprising model from somewhere else.

That fallback rule is now more specific for explicit user choice too. Automatic fallback is acceptable when the system is resolving the default provider and the user did not explicitly choose one for the current request. But if the user explicitly picks Codex, Copilot, or LM Studio and that provider's local config is broken, the product should show an error rather than silently switching to a different provider behind the user's back. That keeps provider choice trustworthy and avoids confusing “I selected Copilot but got Codex” behavior.

The remaining config decision is also now fixed. Each provider's `chat/config.toml` should store both the default model and that provider's default Agent Flags where the repository actually supports them. This makes the provider-local chat config the single source of truth for supported chat defaults instead of repeating the current Codex split where some defaults come from `codex/chat/config.toml`, some come from env vars, and some are still hardcoded. This story therefore includes a small Codex cleanup as well as new Copilot and LM Studio config seeding.

Provider-local chat config changes should be reflected by the next relevant request rather than only after a restart. This matches the current Codex precedent, where defaults are reread through normal request-time resolution instead of a separate file watcher. In practice, that means a user who edits `chat/config.toml` should see the new default behavior on the next matching chat or model/default lookup request without needing a server restart, while the story stays out of scope for background live-watch hot reload.

This story is intentionally focused on normal chat and chat-MCP parity. It should not expand into every other agentic execution surface in one go. General agent runs, flows, and command execution already have their own provider and tooling assumptions and should only be touched where they are required for the chat MCP parity contract being targeted here.

Overall, when this story is complete:

- a user can choose Copilot as the default chat provider through the existing provider-default env path;
- the actual default Copilot model comes from `copilot/chat/config.toml`;
- the chat page shows `Agent Flags` rather than `Codex flags`;
- each provider shows only its supported options;
- Copilot can use tools through the normal chat page and the shared chat MCP interface;
- provider-local chat defaults feel consistent across Codex, Copilot, and LM Studio;
- Copilot auth and runtime state remain safely rooted in `copilot/`;
- valid provider-local chat config files are seeded automatically for Codex, Copilot, and LM Studio in repo-local folders that stay out of Git and out of Docker build context noise.

### Acceptance Criteria

- `CODEINFO_CHAT_DEFAULT_PROVIDER` remains the single top-level environment variable used to choose the preferred default chat provider.
- `CODEINFO_CHAT_DEFAULT_MODEL` is removed from the active chat-default contract for this repository.
- The default chat model for Codex is read from `codex/chat/config.toml`.
- The default chat model for Copilot is read from `copilot/chat/config.toml`.
- The default chat model for LM Studio is read from `lmstudio/chat/config.toml`.
- The provider-local `chat/config.toml` files are treated as repo-owned product defaults seeded by the application, not as proof that every provider natively consumes the same file format itself.
- Each provider's `chat/config.toml` stores both the default model and that provider's default Agent Flags where that provider actually supports them.
- `codex/chat/config.toml`, `copilot/chat/config.toml`, and `lmstudio/chat/config.toml` use one shared product-owned TOML format, with provider-specific fields added only where needed.
- The story includes the small Codex cleanup needed so supported Codex chat defaults are owned by provider-local chat config rather than remaining split across config, env, and hardcoded values.
- The story includes a dependency-refresh pass that updates `@openai/codex` and `@openai/codex-sdk` to the latest published versions verified at implementation time.
- The story rechecks `@github/copilot-sdk` at implementation time and bumps it if a newer published version exists than the currently pinned version.
- The story rechecks `@lmstudio/sdk` at implementation time and only bumps it if a newer release exists than the currently pinned version.
- Copilot auth and runtime state remain rooted in the existing `copilot/` home contract rather than being moved into `copilot/chat/`.
- `copilot/chat/config.toml` is not used as Copilot's runtime `configDir`.
- Copilot plaintext-auth persistence is bootstrapped through `copilot/settings.json` rather than depending on `copilot/config.json` as the primary writable settings source.
- Any Copilot-managed JSON artifact that the server still reads for auth or runtime compatibility is parsed through a dedicated JSONC-capable parser instead of strict `JSON.parse` assumptions.
- Copilot-managed comments or compatibility-only content in `copilot/config.json` do not by themselves block device-auth bootstrap or cause the route to fail before the actual auth flow starts.
- The local bootstrap path seeds `copilot/settings.json` with `storeTokenPlaintext: true` when the file is missing instead of seeding `copilot/config.json` for that setting.
- If the configured default provider is unavailable, the server falls back to the next available provider using that fallback provider's own provider-local default model.
- If a provider-local chat config file is missing, unreadable, or invalid, the server treats that provider as misconfigured for default-model resolution and logs or reports that condition clearly enough for operators to understand why fallback happened.
- If the user explicitly selected a provider for the current request and that provider's local config is broken, the request fails clearly instead of silently switching to another provider.
- The application seeds valid `chat/config.toml` files for Codex, Copilot, and LM Studio rather than requiring users to create those files by hand before chat works correctly.
- The local bootstrap path that creates required repo-local provider folders is extended to create the LM Studio chat-defaults folder.
- The repo `.gitignore` and `.dockerignore` rules keep the repo-local provider folders out of Git and out of Docker build context, including the LM Studio provider folder introduced by this story.
- The new `lmstudio/` folder is treated as an app-managed seeded chat-defaults folder only, not as LM Studio runtime state or a native LM Studio home.
- Changes to provider-local `chat/config.toml` files are picked up on the next relevant request rather than only after a server restart.
- This story does not require a background file watcher or immediate live propagation for provider-local config edits.
- The chat page no longer depends on a Codex-only `Codex flags` concept as its primary provider-options architecture.
- The chat page exposes a provider-neutral `Agent Flags` surface.
- The `Agent Flags` surface is driven by provider capability metadata from the server rather than by hardcoded `provider === 'codex'` checks in the client.
- The provider-capability contract includes enough metadata for the client to render supported options, defaults, and editability without inferring them from provider names.
- Each provider exposes a common model-aware option-descriptor method that returns a JSON array of supported `Agent Flags`.
- Each option-descriptor item includes a stable key, label, UI control type, supported values or bounds, a hardcoded seed default, and the resolved default loaded from the provider's current `chat/config.toml`.
- The client receives Agent Flags through the same combined response that returns the model list for the selected provider.
- That combined models-and-flags response includes common provider flags plus per-model overrides or capability narrowing where needed.
- The client refreshes that combined payload when the user changes provider and uses the selected model's override data when the user changes model.
- The client displays config-resolved defaults from the current provider `chat/config.toml` rather than blindly showing only the hardcoded seed defaults.
- Codex continues to expose its supported chat options through the provider-neutral `Agent Flags` contract, refreshed to match the newer supported Codex config surface chosen for this story.
- The first provider-neutral Codex option set includes sandbox mode, approval policy, reasoning effort, reasoning summary, verbosity, network access, and web search mode.
- Codex `webSearchMode` replaces the old boolean `webSearchEnabled` contract and supports `disabled`, `cached`, and `live`.
- The seeded Codex default for `webSearchMode` is the most permissive supported value, `live`.
- Codex `approvalPolicy` no longer exposes `on-failure` in the editable UI or seeded config for this story.
- Codex granular approval is not required to ship in this story.
- Codex `modelReasoningSummary` is exposed through the provider-neutral option-descriptor contract with the supported values `auto`, `concise`, `detailed`, and `none`.
- Codex `modelVerbosity` is exposed through the provider-neutral option-descriptor contract with the supported values `low`, `medium`, and `high`.
- Codex `networkAccessEnabled` remains a boolean in the product contract and maps to the Codex `sandbox_workspace_write.network_access` setting.
- The seeded Codex default for `networkAccessEnabled` is `true`.
- If the selected Codex sandbox mode is not `workspace-write`, the UI and server contract handle `networkAccessEnabled` as workspace-write-specific rather than pretending it is a provider-wide switch.
- Older local Codex chat configs that still use removed settings are normalized forward with a clear warning rather than breaking chat.
- Copilot exposes a first real editable flag set in this story rather than showing no editable flags.
- Copilot's first supported editable options are limited to real Copilot-aligned controls such as reasoning effort and one tool-access setting.
- The first Copilot tool-access setting supports only `On` and `Off` in this story.
- Copilot does not expose `workingDirectory` as a visible Agent Flag in this story.
- Copilot does not expose approval or permission-mode controls as visible Agent Flags in this story.
- Copilot sessions use a non-interrupting permissive permission-handler default suitable for the repository's containerized environment so supported Copilot tool runs can proceed without repeated approval prompts.
- The first visible Copilot tool-access control is a single product-level setting rather than separate per-server MCP switches.
- LM Studio exposes a first real editable flag set in this story rather than showing no editable flags.
- LM Studio's first supported editable options are limited to honest LM Studio-native generation or tool controls such as temperature, max tokens, context overflow handling, and tool access.
- Copilot exposes only the options that are truly supported by the repository's Copilot integration and the installed SDK path used here.
- LM Studio exposes only the options that are truly supported by the repository's LM Studio integration.
- This story does not require Copilot or LM Studio to expose the same number of editable flags as Codex.
- Changing Agent Flags does not automatically start a new conversation.
- A change to Agent Flags affects later sends in the current conversation rather than clearing the transcript or forcing a fresh draft.
- A provider or model change remains a next-send new-conversation boundary rather than a same-conversation mutation.
- When the user changes provider or model, the active Agent Flags draft is rebuilt from the newly selected provider/model defaults and any now-incompatible hidden flags are cleared from draft state immediately.
- The next send after a provider or model change does not reuse the prior conversation's provider-specific persisted state, including Codex `threadId`.
- If the user restores an existing conversation after staging a provider or model change, the restored conversation's provider, model, and compatible persisted Agent Flags win over the unsent draft state.
- The client no longer sends provider-specific chat options through a `codexFlags`-only request shape as the primary chat-page contract.
- The shared chat request contract becomes provider-neutral enough to carry provider-specific agent options without preserving Codex-only naming as the main abstraction.
- The selected `Agent Flags` values from the chat page are sent with each normal chat request after the user changes them.
- Server-side chat validation continues to reject provider-specific options that are not valid for the selected provider.
- Server-side chat validation rejects contradictory provider/model/Agent-Flags combinations rather than silently coercing them to something else.
- Normal chat execution can create Copilot sessions with repository-managed tools wired in through the existing Copilot session seam.
- Copilot tool wiring uses the repository's own app-managed tool construction path as the primary parity mechanism for this story.
- Copilot tool parity does not depend on native provider-side MCP config discovery being present or enabled.
- The chat page can use Copilot with tools available through the same product-managed tool layer that underpins the supported chat-tool experience elsewhere in the repository.
- The chat MCP interface supports Copilot anywhere the current shared chat-provider contract is expected to accept a provider choice, including the current `codebase_question` path.
- MCP chat requests using Copilot follow the same provider-resolution and validation rules as the normal chat page rather than using a second special-case provider contract.
- The public chat MCP request contract stays limited to provider and model selection in this story and does not add Agent Flags.
- Shared provider and model contracts returned by the server are updated so the client can render provider-neutral defaults and capability data without depending on Codex-only payload fields as the main mechanism.
- Any remaining Codex-specific response fields that still need to exist for compatibility must no longer be the primary way the client decides which provider controls to render.
- Existing Codex behavior remains working after the provider-neutral refactor, including current Codex defaults, model capability handling, and tool-aware chat behavior.
- Existing LM Studio behavior remains working after the provider-neutral refactor, including provider discovery, model loading, and normal chat execution.
- Existing Copilot provider availability, model loading, and conversation execution remain working while gaining the stronger defaults and tool parity described here.
- The documentation and seeded provider config comments explain that `CODEINFO_CHAT_DEFAULT_PROVIDER` chooses the provider and each provider's `chat/config.toml` chooses that provider's default chat model.
- Tests are updated to cover provider-local model defaults, provider fallback using per-provider config files, provider-neutral Agent Flags rendering, Copilot tool wiring, provider-neutral request validation, and Copilot support in the chat MCP interface.

### Out Of Scope

- Replacing provider-specific native runtime configs with one universal provider runtime format.
- Moving Copilot auth or runtime state out of `copilot/` and into `copilot/chat/`.
- Turning the new `lmstudio/` folder into a native LM Studio runtime-state home in this story.
- Making Copilot's native CLI or SDK config discovery the primary product mechanism for tool parity.
- Exposing every possible native Copilot CLI or SDK option in the chat page.
- Exposing Copilot `workingDirectory` as a first-pass visible Agent Flag in this story.
- Exposing Copilot permission or approval controls as a first-pass visible Agent Flag in this story.
- Exposing built-in Copilot MCP servers one by one in the first-pass `Agent Flags` UI for this story.
- Adding a middle Copilot tool-access mode in this story.
- Exposing every possible Codex option from the newer library versions in the first pass of this story.
- Building the newer Codex granular approval structure into the first shipping `Agent Flags` UI for this story.
- Inventing fake Copilot or LM Studio flags purely for UI symmetry with Codex.
- Treating the hardcoded seed defaults and the config-resolved defaults as the same concept in the provider option-descriptor contract.
- Requiring users to hand-create provider-local chat config folders or files before the supported chat providers can work with their seeded defaults.
- Requiring a full restart before provider-local `chat/config.toml` changes can affect later requests.
- Adding a background file-watch hot reload system for provider-local chat config changes.
- Broad refactors of non-chat agent execution surfaces unless a change is directly required for the shared chat MCP provider contract.
- Reworking flow execution, command execution, or agent execution to use the same provider-default architecture in this story unless they are explicitly part of the chat MCP path being updated.
- Adding new provider types beyond Codex, Copilot, and LM Studio.
- Redesigning authentication UX beyond what is needed to keep existing provider auth flows coherent with the stronger chat-provider contract.
- Introducing a second environment variable for default chat model selection after provider-local chat config becomes the source of truth.
- Preserving hidden hardcoded model selection as a silent fallback once provider-local chat config is required for default-model resolution.
- Adding Agent Flags to the public chat MCP request contract in this story.
- Automatically starting a new conversation just because Agent Flags changed.
- Requiring Copilot and LM Studio to expose exactly the same editable controls that Codex exposes.
- Keeping a chat-page UI that still hardcodes provider-specific control rendering instead of using the shared option-descriptor payload as the source of truth.
- Rebuilding unrelated chat transcript presentation outside the work needed to support provider-neutral flags, defaults, and tool parity.

### Additional Repositories

- No Additional Repositories

## Questions

- No Further Questions

## Decisions

1. The chat MCP interface should stay provider/model-defaults-only in this story.
   - The question being addressed: In the chat MCP interface, should requests use only provider and model defaults, or should they also accept Agent Flags?
   - Why the question matters: This determines whether Copilot MCP parity means shared defaults and provider support, or whether the story also has to expand the public MCP request contract with a second flags surface.
   - What the answer is: Keep the chat MCP request contract limited to provider and model selection in this story, and do not add Agent Flags to the public MCP input shape yet. Copilot parity in MCP should come from provider support, shared default resolution, and server-managed tool wiring rather than from exposing every chat-page control through MCP.
   - Where the answer came from: User decision in this planning session, supported by repo evidence from [server/src/mcp2/tools/codebaseQuestion.ts](server/src/mcp2/tools/codebaseQuestion.ts), [planning/0000047-codex-chat-config-defaults-bootstrap-and-context7-overlay.md](planning/0000047-codex-chat-config-defaults-bootstrap-and-context7-overlay.md), and [planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md](planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md), plus external evidence from DeepWiki and GitHub Copilot documentation showing that Copilot can support per-session tools and MCP configuration even when the product keeps its public MCP request contract small.
   - Why it is the best answer: It keeps the story focused, preserves a simple MCP contract, avoids adding a second provider-flags API surface before the provider-neutral chat-page contract is settled, and still delivers the user-facing parity goal that Copilot can be selected and run through the shared chat MCP path.
2. Agent Flag changes should stay in the current conversation.
   - The question being addressed: If a user changes Agent Flags, should the next message stay in the same conversation, or start a new conversation?
   - Why the question matters: This decides whether changing tools or runtime posture resets the visible transcript or simply affects later sends inside the current thread.
   - What the answer is: Keep Agent Flag changes in the same conversation. A flag change should affect later sends in the current conversation rather than automatically starting a fresh draft.
   - Where the answer came from: User decision in this planning session, supported by repo evidence from [client/src/pages/ChatPage.tsx](client/src/pages/ChatPage.tsx), [client/src/hooks/useChatStream.ts](client/src/hooks/useChatStream.ts), and [client/src/test/chatPage.newConversation.test.tsx](client/src/test/chatPage.newConversation.test.tsx), which show that provider and model changes trigger next-send new-conversation behavior while existing flag changes do not.
   - Why it is the best answer: It preserves the current user experience, avoids surprising transcript resets when the user only changes runtime controls, and keeps the provider-neutral Agent Flags refactor aligned with existing chat-page behavior instead of introducing a second conversation-reset rule.
3. Provider-local chat config should store supported default Agent Flags as well as the default model, and the app should seed valid provider folders and config files.
   - The question being addressed: Should each provider's `chat/config.toml` store only the default model, or also that provider's default Agent Flags?
   - Why the question matters: This determines whether provider-local chat defaults become the true single source of truth or whether the repository keeps the current split between config files, env vars, and hardcoded defaults.
   - What the answer is: Each provider's `chat/config.toml` should store both the default model and that provider's default Agent Flags where that provider actually supports them. The application should seed valid provider-local chat config files for Codex, Copilot, and LM Studio, and the local bootstrap shell path should create the LM Studio folder as part of that setup. The repo's gitignore rules should keep those repo-local provider folders out of Git, including the new `lmstudio/` folder.
   - Where the answer came from: User decision in this planning session, supported by repo evidence from [server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts](server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts), [server/src/codex/capabilityResolver.ts](server/src/codex/capabilityResolver.ts), [server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts](server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts), [scripts/docker-compose-with-env.sh](scripts/docker-compose-with-env.sh), and [.gitignore](.gitignore); external evidence from the official Codex config docs and DeepWiki guidance on per-session Copilot configuration, which show that chat defaults can legitimately include more than model selection even when the product chooses to store them in its own provider-local config layer.
   - Why it is the best answer: It removes the current Codex inconsistency, makes provider-local defaults genuinely complete, avoids manual setup steps for users, and keeps runtime/provider folders predictable and out of source control noise.
4. Provider-local config changes should be picked up on the next request.
   - The question being addressed: Should changes to `chat/config.toml` take effect on the next request, or only after restart?
   - Why the question matters: This sets user expectations for how quickly provider-local config edits affect later chat behavior.
   - What the answer is: Pick up `chat/config.toml` changes on the next relevant request rather than only after restart. This story does not require a background watcher; request-time reread is enough.
   - Where the answer came from: User decision in this planning session, supported by repo evidence from [planning/0000047-codex-chat-config-defaults-bootstrap-and-context7-overlay.md](planning/0000047-codex-chat-config-defaults-bootstrap-and-context7-overlay.md), [server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts](server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts), and [server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts](server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts), which already establish request-time reread precedent for Codex chat defaults.
   - Why it is the best answer: It matches current repo behavior, gives quick operator feedback, and avoids expanding this story into a separate hot-reload watcher project.
5. Copilot and LM Studio should define their first real editable Agent Flags now, using a shared option-descriptor contract.
   - The question being addressed: Should Copilot and LM Studio start with no extra editable Agent Flags, or should this story define their first flags now?
   - Why the question matters: This decides whether the new `Agent Flags` surface is mostly a provider-neutral shell around existing Codex controls at first, or whether this story must also define concrete first-wave Copilot and LM Studio options plus the shared metadata contract that the client will render from.
   - What the answer is: Define the first real editable Agent Flags now, but keep them honest to each provider. Each provider should expose a common model-aware option-descriptor method that returns a JSON array of supported options, where each option includes a stable key, label, UI control type, supported values or bounds, a hardcoded seed default, and the resolved default from the current provider `chat/config.toml`. The client should use that descriptor payload to build the `Agent Flags` UI, show config-resolved defaults initially, and send the user's selected values with each normal chat request. Copilot's first supported editable set should be limited to real Copilot-aligned controls such as reasoning effort and one tool-access setting. LM Studio's first supported editable set should be limited to honest LM Studio-native generation or tool controls such as temperature, max tokens, context overflow handling, and tool access rather than copied Codex labels.
   - Where the answer came from: User decision in this planning session, supported by repo evidence from [client/src/components/chat/CodexFlagsPanel.tsx](client/src/components/chat/CodexFlagsPanel.tsx), [client/src/pages/ChatPage.tsx](client/src/pages/ChatPage.tsx), [server/src/chat/interfaces/ChatInterfaceCopilot.ts](server/src/chat/interfaces/ChatInterfaceCopilot.ts), [server/src/chat/interfaces/ChatInterfaceLMStudio.ts](server/src/chat/interfaces/ChatInterfaceLMStudio.ts), [server/src/routes/chatModels.ts](server/src/routes/chatModels.ts), [server/src/lmstudio/tools.ts](server/src/lmstudio/tools.ts), and [node_modules/@lmstudio/sdk/dist/index.d.ts](node_modules/@lmstudio/sdk/dist/index.d.ts); external evidence from DeepWiki on `github/copilot-sdk`, official GitHub Copilot documentation about tool and MCP configuration, and official LM Studio documentation about agent and MCP support, which together show that both providers have real first-wave option surfaces even though they do not map directly to the existing Codex-only flags.
   - Why it is the best answer: It replaces the current Codex-only UI contract with a provider-neutral metadata contract, keeps the rendered controls honest to each provider, preserves the distinction between shipped defaults and config-resolved defaults, and avoids a fake “same as Codex” experience for Copilot or LM Studio.
6. The new `lmstudio/` folder should hold only seeded chat config in this story, not LM Studio runtime state.
   - The question being addressed: Should the new `lmstudio/` folder hold only the seeded chat config, or should it also hold LM Studio runtime state?
   - Why the question matters: This decides whether `lmstudio/` is just an app-managed defaults folder for this story or whether it becomes a broader provider-state home with extra persistence meaning and Docker/runtime implications.
   - What the answer is: Keep the new `lmstudio/` folder limited to app-managed seeded chat config in this story. Do not turn it into LM Studio runtime state, a native LM Studio home, or a place for LM Studio-native MCP/runtime files as part of this work.
   - Where the answer came from: User decision in this planning session, supported by repo evidence from [server/src/routes/lmstudio.ts](server/src/routes/lmstudio.ts), [server/src/lmstudio/clientPool.ts](server/src/lmstudio/clientPool.ts), [server/src/lmstudio/tools.ts](server/src/lmstudio/tools.ts), [scripts/docker-compose-with-env.sh](scripts/docker-compose-with-env.sh), and [planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md](planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md); external evidence was not needed because the ambiguity is about this repository's own folder contract rather than an external SDK requirement.
   - Why it is the best answer: It matches the current LM Studio integration shape in this repository, keeps the story focused on product-owned chat defaults and provider-neutral UI work, and avoids creating a larger LM Studio-native state contract as part of an already broad story.
7. The Codex option contract should be refreshed for the newer library versions, while keeping the first UI pass intentionally simpler than the full new Codex config surface.
   - The question being addressed: How should the Codex and LM Studio library review in this story affect the supported Codex Agent Flags?
   - Why the question matters: Newer Codex releases expose a broader config surface than the current Codex-only UI, so the story needs a clear contract for which new Codex options become supported `Agent Flags` now and which stay out of scope.
   - What the answer is: Update `@openai/codex` and `@openai/codex-sdk` to the latest published versions verified at implementation time, and recheck `@lmstudio/sdk`, bumping it only if a newer release exists then. Refresh the supported Codex Agent Flags so they include `webSearchMode` instead of the old boolean `webSearchEnabled`, add `modelReasoningSummary` and `modelVerbosity`, remove `on-failure` from the supported editable approval options, keep Codex granular approval out of scope, and keep `networkAccessEnabled` as the simple workspace-write network toggle by mapping it directly to `sandbox_workspace_write.network_access`. The seeded Codex defaults should remain permissive for the newly clarified options, including `webSearchMode = live` and `networkAccessEnabled = true`.
   - Where the answer came from: User decision in this planning session, supported by repo evidence from [server/package.json](server/package.json), [client/src/components/chat/CodexFlagsPanel.tsx](client/src/components/chat/CodexFlagsPanel.tsx), and [server/src/routes/chatValidators.ts](server/src/routes/chatValidators.ts); external evidence from the official OpenAI Codex config reference and current npm registry/package data for `@openai/codex`, `@openai/codex-sdk`, and `@lmstudio/sdk`.
   - Why it is the best answer: It lets the story benefit from the newer Codex library surface without turning this release into a full Codex-permissions redesign, preserves a simple product contract where that contract is still honest, and ensures the first provider-neutral Codex metadata is based on the current supported library behavior rather than the older Codex-only UI snapshot.
8. The first-pass Copilot option contract should stay focused on real session controls, while permission handling stays a non-UI default.
   - The question being addressed: Which Copilot options should be exposed in the first provider-neutral `Agent Flags` contract, and should Copilot permission behavior be a visible user option?
   - Why the question matters: The Copilot SDK exposes many session fields, but only some of them are simple and honest enough for an early provider-neutral UI. The product also wants Copilot to run without interruption inside the container, so the plan needs to decide whether that should be user-controlled or implementation-controlled.
   - What the answer is: Keep the first visible Copilot `Agent Flags` limited to reasoning effort and one tool-access setting. Do not expose `workingDirectory` as a visible Copilot flag in this story, because omitting it already falls back to the current process working directory and the product does not need a separate UI override right now. Do not expose Copilot permission or approval mode as a visible flag either. Instead, implement Copilot with a permissive non-interrupting approval default such as the SDK's `approveAll` helper or an equivalent always-approve permission handler so supported tool runs can proceed without repeated prompts in the containerized environment.
   - Where the answer came from: User decision in this planning session, supported by repo evidence from [server/package.json](server/package.json), [server/src/chat/interfaces/ChatInterfaceCopilot.ts](server/src/chat/interfaces/ChatInterfaceCopilot.ts), and [server/src/chat/copilotLifecycle.ts](server/src/chat/copilotLifecycle.ts); external evidence from the current GitHub Copilot SDK documentation and DeepWiki's indexed `github/copilot-sdk` documentation covering `SessionConfig.workingDirectory`, `onPermissionRequest`, and the `approveAll` helper.
   - Why it is the best answer: It keeps the first Copilot UI small and honest, matches the product's desire for non-interrupting Copilot runs inside Docker, and avoids turning this parity story into a large Copilot permission-management design before the basic provider-neutral contract is complete.
9. Older local Codex chat configs should be normalized forward with a warning rather than failing chat.
   - The question being addressed: If an older Codex chat config uses removed settings, should we auto-fix it or stop with an error?
   - Why the question matters: This story removes `on-failure` and replaces `webSearchEnabled` with `webSearchMode`, so existing local Codex chat configs may no longer match the new supported shape.
   - What the answer is: Auto-fix older Codex chat configs and log a warning instead of stopping with an error. A good first implementation is to normalize old values when the config is read, then rewrite the local file on the next safe seed or save path.
   - Where the answer came from: User decision in this planning session, supported by repo evidence from [server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts](server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts), [server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts](server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts), and [planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md](planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md); external evidence from the official OpenAI Codex config reference showing the newer `web_search` and approval contract.
   - Why it is the best answer: It keeps existing local installs usable, matches the repo's preference for seeded defaults and request-time config refresh, and avoids breaking chat just because a user still has an older local file shape.
10. The first Copilot tool control should be one product-level setting, not per-server switches.

- The question being addressed: For Copilot, should users switch the built-in MCP servers one by one, or just use one tool access setting?
- Why the question matters: This decides whether the first Copilot UI stays very simple or gives users finer control over the built-in MCP and tool surface.
- What the answer is: Use one tool-access setting in the first visible Copilot UI. Even though the SDK uses allowlist or denylist arrays rather than one native enum, the product can still expose one tool-access choice and map it to the SDK internally. Per-server MCP switches should stay out of scope for this story.
- Where the answer came from: User decision in this planning session, supported by repo evidence from [server/src/chat/interfaces/ChatInterfaceCopilot.ts](server/src/chat/interfaces/ChatInterfaceCopilot.ts), [server/src/chat/factory.ts](server/src/chat/factory.ts), and [planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md](planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md); external evidence from the current GitHub Copilot SDK documentation and DeepWiki's indexed `github/copilot-sdk` docs showing that a product-level single tool-access control can be mapped realistically onto the SDK's `availableTools` and `excludedTools` fields.
- Why it is the best answer: It keeps the first Copilot UI small, keeps the product contract easy to understand, and still aligns honestly with what the SDK can support under the hood.

11. Agent Flags should load with the model list in one combined response, with common flags plus per-model overrides where needed.

- The question being addressed: Should the page load Agent Flags with the model list, or make a second request just for Agent Flags?
- Why the question matters: The new Agent Flags are model-aware, so the app needs one clear place to return flags, defaults, and models without giving the client mismatched data.
- What the answer is: Load Agent Flags with the model list rather than making a second request. The combined response should return common provider flags once and include per-model overrides or capability narrowing where the selected model changes what the UI should show, such as model-aware reasoning controls.
- Where the answer came from: User decision in this planning session, supported by repo evidence from [server/src/routes/chatModels.ts](server/src/routes/chatModels.ts), [server/src/routes/chatProviders.ts](server/src/routes/chatProviders.ts), [client/src/hooks/useChatModel.ts](client/src/hooks/useChatModel.ts), and [planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md](planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md); external evidence from the official OpenAI Codex config reference and current Copilot SDK documentation showing that some supported options depend on the selected model.
- Why it is the best answer: It keeps the client bootstrap flow simple, avoids a second fetch and a race between model data and flag data, and still preserves the honesty of model-dependent options by letting each model narrow or override the common provider contract.

12. Provider-local chat config should stay in one shared product-owned TOML format.

- The question being addressed: Should all provider chat config files use one shared format, or should each provider have its own format?
- Why the question matters: The story now seeds `codex/chat/config.toml`, `copilot/chat/config.toml`, and `lmstudio/chat/config.toml`, so the file shape needs to be clear before we can build loaders, validation, seeding, and docs.
- What the answer is: Use one shared product-owned format, with provider-specific fields only where needed, and keep that format in TOML. That matches the story's decision that these are app-owned defaults files rather than native provider configs, reduces parser complexity, and makes the seeding and validation path much easier to keep consistent across providers.
- Where the answer came from: User decision in this planning session, supported by repo evidence from [planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md](planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md), [server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts](server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts), and [server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts](server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts); external evidence from the official OpenAI Codex and GitHub Copilot docs showing that the providers already have different native config models, which is one more reason for this repo-owned layer to stay normalized.
- Why it is the best answer: It preserves the current repo convention, avoids three drifting local file contracts, and makes shared seeding, parsing, validation, and documentation much easier to keep correct.

13. Explicit provider choice should fail clearly when that provider's local config is broken.

- The question being addressed: If I pick a provider and its local config is broken, should chat switch providers or show an error?
- Why the question matters: Fallback makes sense for automatic defaults, but a silent provider switch could be surprising if the user explicitly chose Codex, Copilot, or LM Studio on purpose.
- What the answer is: If the user explicitly chose the provider, show an error instead of silently switching providers. Keep automatic fallback only for default-provider resolution when the user did not make a fresh provider choice in that request.
- Where the answer came from: User decision in this planning session, supported by repo evidence from [server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts](server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts), [server/src/routes/chatValidators.ts](server/src/routes/chatValidators.ts), [client/src/pages/ChatPage.tsx](client/src/pages/ChatPage.tsx), and [planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md](planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md); external evidence was not needed because this is primarily a product-behavior decision for this repository.
- Why it is the best answer: It keeps provider choice trustworthy and avoids confusing “I selected Copilot but got Codex” behavior.

14. The first Copilot tool-access control should offer only `On` and `Off`.

- The question being addressed: Should Copilot tool access just be On or Off, or should it include a middle option too?
- Why the question matters: The story now says Copilot gets one tool-access setting, so we still need to decide whether that one setting stays very simple or includes a safer middle mode.
- What the answer is: Start with just `On` and `Off`. `On` maps to the normal allowed tool surface, and `Off` maps to no tool access. A middle mode can be a future story if the product later wants one.
- Where the answer came from: User decision in this planning session, supported by repo evidence from [planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md](planning/0000056-users-can-use-copilot-as-a-first-class-chat-provider-with-shared-agent-flags-and-defaults.md), [server/src/chat/interfaces/ChatInterfaceCopilot.ts](server/src/chat/interfaces/ChatInterfaceCopilot.ts), and [server/src/chat/factory.ts](server/src/chat/factory.ts); external evidence from current GitHub Copilot custom-agents docs and DeepWiki's indexed `github/copilot-sdk` docs showing that “all tools” and “no tools” map naturally onto the SDK even without a native single enum.
- Why it is the best answer: It keeps the first Copilot UI easy to understand, matches the story's choice to avoid per-server switches, and still leaves room for a future middle mode if the product later wants one.

## Runtime And Repo Prerequisites

- The current default-path chat runtime already exists in one repository and should stay the only repository in scope for this story. The standard server boot path in [server/src/index.ts](server/src/index.ts) already wires the chat request route, the chat-provider route, the chat-models route, the MCP tool registry, and the Copilot auth route, so this story should extend those existing paths rather than introducing a second listener, sidecar worker, or additional repository.
- The frontend work also stays on the existing chat surface. The current server boot path already serves the same REST endpoints that [client/src/pages/ChatPage.tsx](client/src/pages/ChatPage.tsx), [client/src/hooks/useChatModel.ts](client/src/hooks/useChatModel.ts), and [client/src/hooks/useChatStream.ts](client/src/hooks/useChatStream.ts) consume, so this story does not require a new backend service, a second API family, or a separate frontend page just to expose provider-neutral Agent Flags.
- Provider-default resolution already lives on the normal server path through [server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts](server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts), [server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts](server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts), and the request/route seams in [server/src/routes/chat.ts](server/src/routes/chat.ts), [server/src/routes/chatProviders.ts](server/src/routes/chatProviders.ts), and [server/src/routes/chatModels.ts](server/src/routes/chatModels.ts). Story work must land on those shared seams so the new provider-neutral behavior is reachable from the normal REST and MCP entrypoints instead of through a story-only bootstrap path.
- Existing startup and environment loading still advertise both `CODEINFO_CHAT_DEFAULT_PROVIDER` and `CODEINFO_CHAT_DEFAULT_MODEL` through [server/src/config/startupEnv.ts](server/src/config/startupEnv.ts), [server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts](server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts), and the current env-loading/config tests. This story must therefore remove the old shared default-model contract everywhere it participates in the normal path, not just in one route or one provider-specific code path.
- Copilot runtime readiness, auth-home discovery, and model availability already flow through [server/src/config/copilotConfig.ts](server/src/config/copilotConfig.ts), [server/src/providers/copilotReadiness.ts](server/src/providers/copilotReadiness.ts), [server/src/chat/copilotLifecycle.ts](server/src/chat/copilotLifecycle.ts), and [server/src/routes/copilotDeviceAuth.ts](server/src/routes/copilotDeviceAuth.ts). The new `copilot/chat/config.toml` file is a repo-owned chat-defaults file only; it must not replace the existing Copilot runtime `configDir`, auth store, or readiness path. The installed `@github/copilot-sdk` contract also makes `onPermissionRequest` mandatory on both `createSession` and `resumeSession`, and exposes `tools`, `availableTools`, `excludedTools`, `workingDirectory`, `configDir`, and `reasoningEffort` as the real session fields this story must map onto rather than inventing a bespoke Copilot-only runtime contract.
- LM Studio chat integration already flows through [server/src/chat/interfaces/ChatInterfaceLMStudio.ts](server/src/chat/interfaces/ChatInterfaceLMStudio.ts), [server/src/lmstudio/tools.ts](server/src/lmstudio/tools.ts), and [server/src/routes/lmstudio.ts](server/src/routes/lmstudio.ts). The new `lmstudio/` folder in this story is therefore only a seeded defaults location and must not become a second LM Studio runtime home, a new listener, or a hidden prerequisite for normal provider availability. The installed `@lmstudio/sdk` contract exposes `temperature`, `maxTokens`, `contextOverflowPolicy`, and `signal` on prediction calls, while the SDK marks `rawTools` on normal `.respond(...)` predictions as deprecated. This story should therefore keep LM Studio tool access on the existing adapter-level execution path instead of introducing a second OpenAI-style internal tool-choice contract.
- The inspected Docker paths already build the server and client from the repository root through [server/Dockerfile](server/Dockerfile), [client/Dockerfile](client/Dockerfile), [docker-compose.yml](docker-compose.yml), and [docker-compose.e2e.yml](docker-compose.e2e.yml), and those compose stacks already advertise `CODEINFO_RUNTIME_SOURCE_BIND_MOUNT_COUNT=0` for the server path. Because source is copied into images and local provider folders can exist on disk before a build, this story must extend [.dockerignore](.dockerignore) so `lmstudio/**` is excluded alongside the existing `codex/**` and `copilot/**` rules instead of introducing a new LM Studio image-time bind mount or volume contract.
- Manual proof should stay on the normal human stack first: `npm run compose:build` and then `npm run compose:up` against [docker-compose.yml](docker-compose.yml). That stack already exposes the existing `server` health endpoint, the client on port `5001`, and the standard chat routes on port `5010`, so this story should not require a new compose file, a new port, or a special manual-only service just to reach the changed behavior.

## Feasibility Proof Pass

### 1. Provider-local defaults and fallback ownership

- Already existing capabilities:
  - Shared request-time defaults resolution already exists in [server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts](server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts), [server/src/routes/chat.ts](server/src/routes/chat.ts), [server/src/routes/chatProviders.ts](server/src/routes/chatProviders.ts), and [server/src/routes/chatModels.ts](server/src/routes/chatModels.ts).
  - Codex chat-default bootstrap already exists in [server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts](server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts), including temp-file seeding and cleanup of partial chat-config writes.
- Missing prerequisite capabilities:
  - A shared multi-provider `<provider>/chat/config.toml` loader and seeding path does not yet exist for Copilot and LM Studio.
  - The old `CODEINFO_CHAT_DEFAULT_MODEL` env fallback still participates in the active default-resolution path and must be removed before provider-local defaults can be the only truth.
- Assumptions currently invalid:
  - The repository does not already have one shared provider-neutral chat-default contract; Codex is still special-cased in the live code.
- Feasibility and sequencing note:
  - Provider-local config loading, fallback rules, and shared seeding must be settled first, because the browser chat path and the MCP chat path both depend on the same provider/model resolution behavior.

### 2. Provider-neutral Agent Flags and Copilot parity

- Already existing capabilities:
  - Copilot readiness, auth diagnostics, and model discovery already exist in [server/src/providers/copilotReadiness.ts](server/src/providers/copilotReadiness.ts), [server/src/chat/copilotLifecycle.ts](server/src/chat/copilotLifecycle.ts), and [server/src/routes/chatModels.ts](server/src/routes/chatModels.ts).
  - Conversation persistence already has a flat `flags` seam in [server/src/mongo/conversation.ts](server/src/mongo/conversation.ts).
- Missing prerequisite capabilities:
  - The normal chat request and response contracts are still Codex-shaped, and the MCP `codebase_question` provider contract still excludes Copilot.
  - Copilot normal-chat tool parity still needs to be wired through the repository-managed tool path rather than relying on provider-native discovery.
  - The currently installed `@openai/codex-sdk` thread options, and the published `0.125.0` SDK types rechecked for this story, expose `sandboxMode`, `approvalPolicy`, `modelReasoningEffort`, `networkAccessEnabled`, `webSearchMode`, and legacy `webSearchEnabled`, but they still do not expose dedicated thread-option fields for reasoning summary or verbosity. Story work must therefore treat `modelReasoningSummary` and `modelVerbosity` as outward product fields that only ship once their exact adapter mapping is verified against the bumped Codex runtime, rather than assuming the current SDK already has matching option names.
- Assumptions currently invalid:
  - The current `codexFlags` request shape and `codexDefaults` response shape cannot simply be reused unchanged for multi-provider Agent Flags.
- Feasibility and sequencing note:
  - Shared server contracts need to become provider-neutral before the client panel is renamed, so the later tasking pass can update validation, persistence, UI rendering, and MCP behavior in one coherent order.

## Persistence And Cleanup

- [server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts](server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts) currently owns Codex chat-config seeding through `ensureChatRuntimeConfigBootstrapped`, which writes a temp file, renames it into place, and removes partial output through `cleanupPartialChatConfig` when the seed fails. [server/src/config/copilotConfig.ts](server/src/config/copilotConfig.ts) already uses the same temp-write and rename pattern for `copilot/config.json`. The new `copilot/chat/config.toml` and `lmstudio/chat/config.toml` seed/update paths should reuse that same temp-write, rename, and temp-file cleanup contract instead of introducing direct in-place writes for provider chat defaults.
- Request-time reread ownership should stay explicit. [server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts](server/src/config/chatDefaults.ts), [server/src/routes/chat.ts](server/src/routes/chat.ts), [server/src/routes/chatProviders.ts](server/src/routes/chatProviders.ts), [server/src/routes/chatModels.ts](server/src/routes/chatModels.ts), and [server/src/mcp2/tools/codebaseQuestion.ts](server/src/mcp2/tools/codebaseQuestion.ts) are the active readers that should observe edited provider-local defaults on the next relevant request without a watcher or restart-only cache.
- Failed bootstrap or normalization work must not delete a previously valid provider config. Cleanup should target only the temp file or a newly created partial target for the write that failed, so request-time readers never see half-written defaults and operators do not lose a last-known-good config because a refresh failed midway.
- The read-after-write ordering boundary must stay explicit: a provider config writer finishes the temp write first, renames only once the new content is complete, and only then becomes visible to the request-time readers above. Later proof should validate that readers either see the previous good config or the fully written new config, never a partially written middle state.

## Lifecycle And Ordering

- The normal chat route in [server/src/routes/chat.ts](server/src/routes/chat.ts) already has a real lifecycle boundary around conversation lock acquisition, inflight registration, provider execution, and final cleanup. Story changes must preserve that order so provider-default resolution and provider-neutral Agent Flags happen before provider execution starts, and `bridge.cleanup()`, inflight cleanup, pending-cancel cleanup, and lock release still happen in the `finally` path on success, stop, and failure.
- The Copilot runtime path in [server/src/chat/interfaces/ChatInterfaceCopilot.ts](server/src/chat/interfaces/ChatInterfaceCopilot.ts) and [server/src/chat/copilotLifecycle.ts](server/src/chat/copilotLifecycle.ts) already has a create-versus-resume split. Story work must keep create, resume, steady-state event streaming, and final `lifecycle.stop()` teardown explicit, and must register the permission handler, hooks, and repository-managed tools before the first provider event for both create and resume paths.
- Cancellation ordering matters on the client and server boundary. [client/src/hooks/useChatStream.ts](client/src/hooks/useChatStream.ts) already distinguishes explicit stop intent from later `cancel_ack` handling, and [server/src/routes/chat.ts](server/src/routes/chat.ts) already distinguishes stopped from failed terminal cleanup. Later tasking should keep those states separate so a stopped run does not look like a failure and a stale stop request does not leak into the next send.

## Log Or Proof Markers

- Provider-local config bootstrap marker: `DEV_0000047_T03_CHAT_CONFIG_BOOTSTRAP` from [server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts](server/src/config/runtimeConfig.ts)
  - Expected outcome: logs whether the provider chat config already existed or was newly seeded, which proves the bootstrap path ran without relying on host-side file inspection.
- Provider contract marker: `story.0000051.task01.provider_contract_applied` from [server/src/routes/chatProviders.ts](server/src/routes/chatProviders.ts)
  - Expected outcome: shows the ordered provider contract returned by `/chat/providers`, which is the clearest existing marker for default-provider ordering and fallback visibility.
- Models/defaults marker: `story.0000051.task06.models_mapped` plus `DEV_0000040_T07_REST_DEFAULTS_APPLIED` from [server/src/routes/chatModels.ts](server/src/routes/chatModels.ts)
  - Expected outcome: proves Copilot models were normalized into the shared response shape and that the selected provider/model defaults were applied on the REST discovery path.
- Copilot readiness marker: `story.0000051.task05.readiness_evaluated` from [server/src/providers/copilotReadiness.ts](server/src/providers/copilotReadiness.ts)
  - Expected outcome: records whether Copilot was blocked by connectivity, authentication, models, or tools, which keeps readiness failures concrete during later proof runs.
- Chat request-resolution markers: `DEV-0000035:T1:defaults_resolution_evaluated` and `DEV-0000035:T1:defaults_resolution_result` from [server/src/routes/chat.ts](server/src/routes/chat.ts)
  - Expected outcome: distinguishes explicit-provider failures from default-provider fallback behavior on the real send path instead of only on discovery endpoints.

## Message Contracts And Storage Shapes

- The current normal chat request path is still Codex-shaped. [client/src/hooks/useChatStream.ts](client/src/hooks/useChatStream.ts) builds a payload with `provider`, `model`, `messages`, and `codexFlags`, [server/src/routes/chatValidators.ts](server/src/routes/chatValidators.ts) validates that same Codex-only flag family, and [server/src/routes/chat.ts](server/src/routes/chat.ts) only forwards those runtime flags when the execution provider is Codex. This story must replace that Codex-only naming with a provider-neutral request contract for normal chat while keeping validation strict enough to reject unsupported provider-specific options instead of silently forwarding them.
- The concrete normal chat request body should preserve the stable transport fields that already exist today: `provider`, `model`, `message`, `conversationId`, and optional `inflightId`, `threadId`, and `working_folder`. This story should move provider-specific runtime options under one provider-neutral `agentFlags` object rather than leaving them as top-level Codex-only fields, so the shared request shape becomes explicit without renaming the rest of the transport contract unnecessarily.
- Provider and model discovery are currently split between [server/src/routes/chatProviders.ts](server/src/routes/chatProviders.ts), [server/src/routes/chatModels.ts](server/src/routes/chatModels.ts), and [client/src/hooks/useChatModel.ts](client/src/hooks/useChatModel.ts). The story's combined provider-model-flags response must extend the existing `ChatModelsResponse` and `ChatModelInfo` style in [common/src/lmstudio.ts](common/src/lmstudio.ts) rather than inventing a second payload family. In practice, that means keeping `provider`, `available`, `toolsAvailable`, `models`, and `reason`, then adding provider-level Agent Flag descriptors plus per-model overrides or capability narrowing in the same response.
- The current shared model payload already has a real external seam to mirror for Copilot reasoning controls. The installed `@github/copilot-sdk` `ModelInfo` contract exposes `id`, `name`, `supportedReasoningEfforts`, and `defaultReasoningEffort`, and the repository already maps those fields in [server/src/routes/chatModels.ts](server/src/routes/chatModels.ts). The new combined response should keep using those same normalized model concepts so the provider-neutral Agent Flags contract does not invent alternate names for the same Copilot capability data.
- Conversation storage already persists provider-specific runtime metadata as a flat `flags: Record<string, unknown>` on [server/src/mongo/conversation.ts](server/src/mongo/conversation.ts), with request-time merges in [server/src/routes/chat.ts](server/src/routes/chat.ts) and [server/src/mcp2/tools/codebaseQuestion.ts](server/src/mcp2/tools/codebaseQuestion.ts). This story should keep that flat storage shape, store provider-neutral Agent Flag keys there using the same stable names used on the wire, and continue to strip Codex-only `threadId` state whenever the conversation is persisted under a non-Codex provider.
